تخفيف: قال في "البزازية" إن لم يغير المعنى نحو : وقتلُوا تقتيلا لا يفسد، وإن غير نحو: بِرَبِّ النَّاسِ وَظَلَّلْنَا عَلَيْهِمُ الْغَمَامَ (الأعراف: (١٦٠) ، وإِنَّ النَّفْسَ لَأَمَّارَةٌ بِالسُّوءِ (يوسف: ٥٣): اختلفوا، والعامة على يفسد اهـ، وفي "الفتح" عامة المشايخ على أن ترك المد والتشديد كالخطأ في الإعراب فلذا قال كثير بالفساد في الْعَالَمِينَ ، وإِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ لأن "إيا" مخففا الشمس، والأصح لا يفسد، وهو لغة قليلة في إيا المشددة.(رد المحتار) وعكسه فلو قرأ: "فعينا" بالتشديد لا تفسد. وفك المدغم كقولنا: اهدنا الصراط، بإظهار اللام (مراقي الفلاح نحو أن إلخ كنصب همزة العلماء وضم هاء الجلالة في قوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا يَخْشَى اللَّهَ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ الْعُلَمَاءُ) (فاطر (۲۸). لا يفسد قال قاضي خان: وما قاله المتأخرون أوسع، وما قاله المتقدمون أحوط.
In this chapter are issues: First: Mistake in i'rab (grammatical inflection). This includes lightening of a shaddah (doubled letter) and its opposite, shortening of a madd (elongated vowel) and its opposite, separation of an idgham (assimilated letter) and its opposite. If the meaning does not change, the prayer is not invalid by consensus, as in al-Mudmarat. If the meaning changes, such as reciting 'And when Ibrahim his Lord tested' with raising 'Ibrahim' and accusative 'his Lord', the correct view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is invalidation. By analogy to Abu Yusuf's statement, it does not invalidate because he does not consider i'rab, and this is given as fatwa. The later scholars – like Muhammad ibn Muqatil, Muhammad ibn Salam, Isma'il al-Zahid, Abu Bakr Sa'id al-Balkhi, al-Hinduwani, Ibn al-Fadl, and al-Halwani – agreed that a mistake in i'rab does not invalidate at all, even if its belief is kufr, because most people cannot distinguish between different forms of i'rab, and insisting on correctness in i'rab would cause hardship, which is removed by Sharia. Al-Khulasa followed this, and al-Nawazil says it does not invalidate at all, and this is the fatwa. This should be the case when it is an error or mistake and he does not know, or he intentionally does it with something that does not change the meaning much, like putting nasb on 'al-Rahman' in the verse 'al-Rahman over the Throne established'. But if he intentionally does something that changes the meaning much or constitutes kufr, then invalidity is at least the case, and the fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's statement. As for lightening of a shaddah: like reciting 'Iyyaka na'budu' or 'al-'alamin' with lightening. The later scholars said it does not invalidate absolutely without exception according to the chosen view, because leaving madd and tashdid is akin to a mistake in i'rab, as in Qadi Khan. This is the most correct, as in al-Mudmarat... The rule of strengthening a lightened letter is like the opposite in dispute and detail. Similarly, pronouncing an idgham clearly and its opposite – all are one type, as in al-Halabi. Al-Bazzaziyya said: if the meaning does not change, like 'wa qattalu taqtila' it does not invalidate; if it changes like 'bi-rabbi al-nas' and 'wazallalna 'alayhim al-ghamam' and 'inna al-nafsa la-amaratun bi al-su'', they differed, and the majority say it invalidates. In al-Fath, most shaykhs consider leaving madd and tashdid like a mistake in i'rab, so many said it invalidates in 'al-'alamin' and 'iyyaka na'budu' because 'iya' with lightening is the sun, but the correct view is it does not invalidate, and it is a rare dialect for 'iyya' with shaddah. The opposite: if he recites 'fa'aynan' with tashdid, it does not invalidate. Separating idgham: like saying 'ihdina al-sirata' by pronouncing the lam clearly, does not invalidate. Qadi Khan said: what the later scholars said is broader, and what the earlier scholars said is more cautious.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage details the first specific issue: mistakes in i'rab (grammatical inflection). It explains that if the meaning does not change, there is consensus that the prayer does not invalidate. If the meaning does change, there is a dispute: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the prayer invalidates; but according to Abu Yusuf, it does not, because he does not consider i'rab. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) agreed that i'rab mistakes never invalidate, even if the mistaken reading implies kufr, due to the widespread hardship that would result if people were required to have perfect i'rab. This is the relied-upon fatwa. The text then extends this ruling to similar errors: lightening a shaddah, shortening a madd, separating an idgham, etc. For example, reciting 'Iyyaka na'budu' with a lightened ya' (instead of shaddah) does not invalidate, because it is like an i'rab mistake. The text notes that some earlier scholars considered such mistakes invalidating, but the later, more accommodating view is preferred. Qadi Khan summarizes: the later scholars' position is broader (easier), while the earlier scholars' position is more cautious.

Key Terms
الخطأ في الإعراب (al-khaṭ' fī al-i'rāb): A mistake in grammatical inflection (e.g., wrong case ending) during recitation.
تخفيف المشدد (takhfīf al-mushaddad): Lightening a doubled consonant (shaddah) by pronouncing it as single.
رفع الحرج (raf' al-haraj): Removal of hardship; a key principle used to justify leniency in i'rab rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling on a mistake in i'rab (grammatical inflection) during prayer?
A) It always invalidates the prayer
B) It never invalidates the prayer
C) It invalidates only if it changes the meaning
D) It invalidates only if it is intentional

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa holds that i'rab mistakes and analogous errors in recitation do not invalidate prayer, even if they change the meaning, due to the principle of removing hardship.

Errors in I'rab and Their Effect on Prayer Validity
Ruling on i'rab errors and their effect on prayer validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on i'rab errors in prayer and identify when the prayer becomes invalid according to the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Errors in i'rab during prayer do not invalidate by later Hanafi consensus, except for intentional major distortion.

This lesson examines the fiqh of recitation mistakes during salah, specifically errors in grammatical inflection (i'rab) and related issues like tashdid and madd. While earlier Hanafi authorities held that such errors invalidate the prayer if meaning changes, the later consensus—now the mufta bihi—adopts leniency to avoid hardship. Understanding this ruling clarifies a common point of confusion and illustrates how the school balances textual integrity with practical ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the base ruling
What is the act under consideration? Recitation mistake (zallat al-qari') during prayer. First, note the consensus: if the meaning does not change, the prayer remains valid by all.
2
Distinguish when meaning changes
If the i'rab error changes the meaning, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The earlier view (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rules invalidation; Abu Yusuf separately held non-invalidation because i'rab is not essential.
The later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) agreed on non-invalidation absolutely.
3
Understand the later scholars' reasoning
Why did later scholars override the earlier view? Because most people cannot master i'rab, and requiring correctness would cause hardship (haraj), which Sharia removes. They cited ijma of later authorities.
This reasoning extends to tashdid, madd, and idgham errors.
4
Note the exception for intentional major change
Even according to the mufta bihi, if a person deliberately makes an error that drastically changes the meaning or implies kufr, the prayer is invalidated. The mufta bihi in that case is Abu Yusuf's qawl (non-invalidation is not applied).
This preserves the sanctity of Quranic recitation.
5
Apply to related errors
The same principle applies to highlighting a doubled letter (takhfif al-mushaddad), shortening a long vowel, separating an idgham, and their opposites. All are treated like i'rab errors: no invalidation by mufta bihi.
Some minor differences exist but the overall ruling is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الأولى: الخطأ في الإعراب ويدخل فيه تخفيف المشدد، وعكسه، وقصر الممدود، وعكسه، وفك المدغم وعكسه، فإن لم يتغير به المعنى لا تفسد به صلاته بالإجماع كما في "المضمرات"، وإذا تغير المعنى نحو أن يقرأ: ﴿وَإِذِ ابْتَلَى إِبْرَاهِيمَ رَبُّهُ﴾ برفع "إبراهيم" ونصب "ربه" فالصحيح عنهما: الفساد، وعلى قياس قول أبي يوسف : لا تفسد ؛ لأنه لا يعتبر الإعراب وبه يفتى وأجمع المتأخرون كـ محمد ابن مقاتل، ومحمد بن سلام، وإسماعيل الزاهد، وأبي بكر سعيد البلخي، والهندواني وابن الفضل والحلواني على أن الخطأ في الإعراب لا يفسد مطلقا، وإن كان مما اعتقاده كفر ؛ لأن أكثر الناس لا يميزون بين وجوه الإعراب، و في اختيار الصواب في الإعراب إيقاع الناس في الحرج، وهو مرفوع شرعا. وعلى هذا مشى في "الخلاصة"، فقال: وفي "النوازل": لا تفسد في الكل، وبه يفتى، وينبغي أن يكون هذا في ما إذا كان خطأ أو غلطا وهو لا يعلم، أو تعمد ذلك مع ما لا يغير المعنى كثيرا كنصب الرحمن في قوله تعالى: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى (طه: ٥)، أما لو تعمد مع ما يغير المعنى كثيرا، أو يكون اعتقاده كفرا فالفساد حينئذ أقل الأحوال، والمفتى به قول أبي يوسف له، وأما تخفيف المشدد: كما لو قرأ إِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ "أو الْعَالَمِينَ بالتخفيف، فقال المتأخرون: لا تفسد مطلقاً من استثناء على المختار؛ لأن ترك المد والتشديد بمنزلة الخطأ في الإعراب كما في قاضي خان"، وهو الأصح كما في "المضمرات"، وكذا نص في "الذخيرة" على أنه الأصح كما في ابن أمير الحاج، وحكم تشديد المخفف كحكم عكسه في الخلاف والتفصيل، وكذا إظهار المدغم وعكسه فالكل نوع واحد كما في "الحلبي". غير في الإعراب: الأولى التعبير بالحركات؛ ليشمل حركات البيئة ككسر قواما مكان فتحها، وفتح باء تعبدي مكان ضمها؛ فإنها لا تفسد حيث لم يغير المعنى (الطحطاوي على الدر). تخفيف: قال في "البزازية" إن لم يغير المعنى نحو: وقتلُوا تقتيلا لا يفسد، وإن غير نحو: بِرَبِّ النَّاسِ وَظَلَّلْنَا عَلَيْهِمُ الْغَمَامَ (الأعراف: ١٦٠) ، وإِنَّ النَّفْسَ لَأَمَّارَةٌ بِالسُّوءِ (يوسف: ٥٣): اختلفوا، والعامة على يفسد اهـ، وفي "الفتح" عامة المشايخ على أن ترك المد والتشديد كالخطأ في الإعراب فلذا قال كثير بالفساد في الْعَالَمِينَ ، وإِيَّاكَ نَعْبُدُ لأن "إيا" مخففا الشمس، والأصح لا يفسد، وهو لغة قليلة في إيا المشددة.(رد المحتار) وعكسه فلو قرأ: "فعينا" بالتشديد لا تفسد. وفك المدغم كقولنا: اهدنا الصراط، بإظهار اللام (مراقي الفلاح نحو أن إلخ كنصب همزة العلماء وضم هاء الجلالة في قوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا يَخْشَى اللَّهَ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ الْعُلَمَاءُ) (فاطر: ٢٨). لا يفسد قال قاضي خان: وما قاله المتأخرون أوسع، وما قاله المتقدمون أحوط.
First issue: Error in grammatical inflection (i'rab), and included in it is lightening of a doubled letter, and its opposite, shortening of a long vowel, and its opposite, separating an idgham and its opposite. If the meaning does not change, his prayer is not invalidated by it by consensus, as in 'al-Mudmarat'. If the meaning changes, such as reciting 'When his Lord tested Ibrahim' with 'Ibrahim' in nominative and 'his Lord' in accusative, the correct from the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is invalidation. By analogy with Abu Yusuf's statement: it is not invalidated because inflection is not considered, and that is given fatwa. And the later scholars like Muhammad ibn Muqatil, Muhammad ibn Salam, Isma'il al-Zahid, Abu Bakr Sa'id al-Balkhi, al-Hinduwani, Ibn al-Fadl, and al-Halwani agreed that error in inflection does not invalidate at all, even if its belief is kufr, because most people cannot distinguish between types of inflection, and insisting on correct inflection would cause hardship, and hardship is removed by Sharia. Based on this the 'Khulasa' proceeded, saying: In 'al-Nawazil': it does not invalidate in all cases, and this is given fatwa. This should be when it is an error or mistake and he does not know, or he deliberately does it with something that does not change the meaning much, like putting 'al-Rahman' in the accusative in the verse 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa'. As for if he deliberately does it with something that changes the meaning much or if his belief is kufr, then invalidation is the least of the cases, and the mufta bihi is the statement of Abu Yusuf. As for lightening a doubled letter: as if he recites 'iyyaka na'budu' or 'al-'alamin' with lightening, the later scholars said: it does not invalidate at all, without exception, on the chosen position, because leaving elongation and tashdid is like error in inflection, as in Qadikhan. That is the sahih as in 'al-Mudmarat'. Similarly it is نص في 'al-Dhakhira' that it is the sahih as in Ibn Amir al-Haj. The ruling of tashdid of a lightened letter is like the ruling of its opposite in terms of ikhtilaf and detail. Similarly, manifesting mughan and its opposite, all are one type as in 'al-Halabi'. Other than in i'rab: the preferred expression is by vowel marks to include environmental vowels like kasr of 'qiwaman' instead of its fatha, and fatha of 'ba' of 'na'budu' instead of its damma; it does not invalidate as long as meaning does not change (al-Tahtawi on al-Durr). Lightening: 'al-Bazzaziyya' said: if it does not change meaning like 'wa qattalu taqtila' it does not invalidate; if it changes meaning like 'bi-rabbi al-nas' and 'wa zallalna 'alayhim al-ghamam' and 'inna al-nafsa la-ammaratun bi al-su': they differed, and the general opinion is that it invalidates. In 'al-Fath' most shaykhs say that leaving madd and tashdid is like error in i'rab, so many say invalidation in 'al-'alamin' and 'iyyaka na'budu' because 'iyya' is the lightened form of the sun, but the sahih is it does not invalidate, and it is a rare language in the tashdid of 'iyya'. (Radd al-Muhtar) Its opposite: if he recites 'fa'aynan' with tashdid, it does not invalidate. Separating idgham like saying 'ihdina al-sirat' with manifesting the lam... it does not invalidate. Qadikhan said: What the later scholars said is more expansive, and what the earlier scholars said is more cautious.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of 'Zallat al-Qari'' (Mistakes of the Reader) in fiqh, detailing the first issue: errors in i'rab (grammatical endings) and related matters like tashdid, madd, idgham. The author first states that if the mistake does not change the meaning, the prayer is valid by consensus, quoting 'al-Mudmarat'. If the meaning changes, then there is a known dispute: The earlier position (from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) said it invalidates, but Abu Yusuf had a separate qawl that it does not invalidate because i'rab is not considered essential. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirin) – named individuals like Muhammad ibn Muqatil, etc. – agreed that errors in i'rab never invalidate, even if the meaning changes, because most people cannot distinguish correct endings, and requiring it would cause hardship (haraj) which is removed by Sharia. This is the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi). However, there is a condition: if the person intentionally makes a mistake that changes the meaning significantly or leads to kufr belief, then the least that can be said is that it invalidates. The mufta bihi there is Abu Yusuf's qawl (not the invalidation). Then the passage extends the same principle to lightening of mushaddad (doubled letter) and its opposite, shortening of mamdud, separating mudgham: the later scholars unanimously say it does not invalidate, because leaving tashdid/madd is like i'rab error. The marginal notes from al-Bazzaziyya and al-Fath add nuance: if it changes meaning like 'bi-rabbi al-nas' (making 'rabb' light), there is difference but the general opinion is invalidation, but the sahih is it does not invalidate. The lesson ends with Qadikhan's comment that the later view is broader, earlier view is more precautionary. The overall message for the student: the established fatwa in the Hanafi school is leniency in such mistakes, following the later scholars, except in clear intentional corruption.

Key Terms
زلة القارئ (zallat al-qāri'): Mistake made by a reciter in the Quran during prayer
الإعراب (al-i'rāb): Grammatical inflection; the vowel endings of words indicating case/gender/number
تخفيف المشدد (takhfīf al-mushaddad): Lightening a doubled letter, i.e., pronouncing it as single

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, if a person recites 'الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ' with a grammatical error that does not change the meaning, what is the ruling on the prayer?
A) The prayer is invalid because any error nullifies it.
B) The prayer is valid because errors in i'rab do not invalidate by fatwa.
C) The prayer is invalid unless the person intended the correct meaning.
D) The prayer is valid only if the error is in tashdid, not i'rab.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the dominant fatwa is that i'rab errors and similar recitation mistakes do not invalidate prayer, unless intentionally altering meaning drastically.

Incorrect Stopping and Starting in Recitation
Ruling on stopping and starting at wrong places in Quran recitation during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on incorrect waqf and ibtida' in prayer and explain the legal basis of hardship removal.

Core Idea
Incorrect waqf or ibtida' in prayer does not invalidate, even if meaning changes—hardship is removed.

This passage addresses a common concern: what if a person stops or starts at a wrong place while reciting the Quran in prayer? Many might worry that such mistakes invalidate the prayer. However, the Hanafi school takes a lenient view based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). Let us explore the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling on: stopping (waqf) and starting (ibtida') at improper places during Quran recitation in prayer. The ruling is that it does not invalidate the prayer.
The act is not a pillar or condition; it is a mistake in performance.
2
Differentiate between meaning change and no meaning change
The passage states that if the meaning does not change, there is consensus on non-invalidation. If the meaning changes, there is difference (ikhtilaf) but the fatwa is still non-invalidation.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of the ruling.
3
Recognize the basis for the ruling
The basis is the removal of hardship (haraj), especially for laypeople. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said hardship is removed.
This principle overrides concerns about meaning distortion.
4
Apply to specific examples
The passage gives examples: cutting a word mid-letter, stopping on a condition before its result, etc. All are ruled non-invalidating.
These examples illustrate the practical scope of the ruling.
5
Note the authority weight
The fatwa is from later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) and is considered sahih (sound). It is the operative position for fatwa.
This shows the development and settled nature of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في الوقف والابتداء في غير موضعهما، فإن لم يتغير به المعنى لا تفسد بالإجماع من المتقدمين والمتأخرين، وإن تغير به المعنى ففيه اختلاف والفتوى على عدم الفساد بكل حال، وهو قول عامة علمائنا المتأخرين؛ لأن في مراعاة الوقف والوصل إيقاع الناس في الحرج لا سيما العوام، والحرج مرفوع، كما في "الذخيرة" و"السراجية" و"النصاب"، وفيه أيضًا لو ترك الوقف في جميع القرآن لا تفسد صلاته عندنا، وأما الحكم في قطع بعض الكلمة كما لو أراد أن يقول: ﴿الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ فقال: "ال"، فوقف على "اللام"، أو على "الحاء"، أو على "الميم"، أو أراد أن يقرأ وَالْعَادِيَاتِ فقال: "والعا" فوقف على "العين"، لانقطاع نفسه أو نسيان الباقي، ثم تمم، أو انتقل إلى آية أخرى، فالذي عليه عامة المشايخ عدم الفساد مطلقا وإن غير المعنى؛ للضرورة وعموم البلوى، كما في "الذخيرة"، وهو الأصح، كما ذكره أبو الليث. في غير موضعهما قال في "البزازية": الابتداء إن كان لا يغير المعنى تغيرا فاحشا لا يفسد، نحو: الوقف على الشرط قبل الجزاء، والابتداء بالجزاء، وكذا بين الصفة والموصوف، وإن غير المعنى نحو: وشَهِدَ اللَّهُ أَنَّهُ لا إِلَهَ (آل عمران: ١٨) ثم ابتدأ إلَّا هُوَ لا يفسد عند عامة المشايخ؛ لأن العوام لا يميزون، ولو وقف على وَقَالَتِ الْيَهُودُ ثم ابتدأ بما بعده لا تفسد بالإجماع. (رد المحتار)
Second issue: Stopping and starting in other than their proper places. If the meaning does not change, it does not invalidate by consensus of the early and later scholars. If the meaning changes, there is difference of opinion, and the fatwa is on non-invalidation in any case. That is the statement of the general body of our later scholars, because observing stopping and connecting would cause hardship for people, especially laymen, and hardship is removed. As in 'al-Dhakhira', 'al-Sirajiyya', and 'al-Nisab'. Likewise, if he leaves stopping in the entire Quran, his prayer does not invalidate according to us. As for the ruling on cutting part of a word, as if he wanted to say 'al-hamdu lillah' and said 'al', then stopped on the 'lam', or on the 'ha', or on the 'mim', or he wanted to read 'wal-'adiyat' and said 'wal-'a', then stopped on the 'ayn', due to his breath running out or forgetting the rest, then he completed or moved to another verse, the position of the general body of shaykhs is non-invalidation absolutely, even if it changes the meaning, due to necessity and general prevalence (of such occurrences), as in 'al-Dhakhira', and that is the sahih, as Abu al-Layth mentioned. For stopping in other than its proper places, al-Bazzaziyya said: Starting, if it does not change the meaning in an extreme way, does not invalidate, such as stopping on the condition before the result, and starting with the result, and similarly between the adjective and the described. If it changes the meaning, such as 'wa shahida Allahu annahu la ilaha' then he starts at 'illa huwa', it does not invalidate according to the general body of shaykhs, because laymen cannot distinguish. And if he stops on 'wa qalati al-yahudu' and then starts with what follows, it does not invalidate by consensus. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage covers the second mas'alah: stopping (waqf) and starting (ibtida') at wrong places during Quran recitation in prayer. The ruling is clearly based on ease for the common person. There is consensus among all scholars (early and later) that if the meaning does not change, the prayer remains valid. If the meaning changes, there was some disagreement, but the established fatwa (al-fatwa) is that it does not invalidate in any case. The reason given is that requiring precise stopping and starting would cause hardship (haraj), especially for laymen, and haraj is removed by Sharia. This is supported by multiple works: al-Dhakhira, al-Sirajiyya, al-Nisab. The passage also extends the ruling to the case of cutting a word (qat' ba'd al-kalima): if a person part of a word due to running out of breath or forgetfulness, then completes it or moves to another verse, the prayer is not invalidated absolutely, even if meaning changes. This is because such occurrences are common and the general public is often unable to avoid them. The marginal note from al-Bazzaziyya adds specific examples: stopping on the condition before the result, or starting with the result without the condition, does not invalidate if the change of meaning is not extreme; even if extreme, most shaykhs say it does not invalidate because laymen cannot distinguish. The note also mentions that stopping on 'wa qalati al-yahudu' and then starting with the next verse (which would imply the Jews said something they didn't) does not invalidate by consensus. The underlying principle is the removal of hardship and the widespread nature of such mistakes.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): Stopping or pausing in recitation at a point that may or may not be a natural pause
الابتداء (al-ibtidā'): Starting recitation from a point, often after a stop
الحرج (al-ḥaraj): Hardship or difficulty that the Sharia aims to remove

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school, if a person stops at 'وَقَالَتِ الْيَهُودُ' and then starts with 'يَدُ اللَّهِ مَغْلُولَةٌ' (which changes meaning), what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is invalid because it implies a false meaning.
B) The prayer is valid because such mistakes do not invalidate.
C) The prayer is invalid only if done intentionally.
D) The prayer is valid if the person immediately corrects.

Key Takeaway
Incorrect stopping or starting in Quran recitation does not invalidate prayer in the Hanafi school, due to the principle of hardship removal.

Substituting One Letter for Another in Recitation
Ruling on replacing letters in Quranic words during prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of substituting letters in Quranic recitation and determine the validity of prayer according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Substitution invalidates prayer only if the word departs from Quranic form AND the meaning changes.

In this lesson, we examine the third mas'alah from Nur al-Idah: substituting one letter for another in Quranic recitation during prayer. The Hanafi school distinguishes three scenarios based on whether the word remains Quranic and whether the meaning changes. Additionally, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between the sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a separate narration of Abu Yusuf. We will unpack each case and see how later scholars relied on the earlier position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three combinations: (1) word remains Quranic, meaning unchanged; (2) word departs from Quranic, meaning unchanged; (3) word remains Quranic, meaning changes.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For scenario 1, prayer is valid by agreement. For scenario 2, the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) rule valid, but Abu Yusuf in another narration says invalid. For scenario 3, the difference is reversed: the two rule invalid, Abu Yusuf (in the other narration) says valid.
The ruling in scenario 2 and 3 depends on which narration is followed.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The disagreement is between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a separate view attributed to Abu Yusuf. The text also mentions later scholars who propose different criteria, but they are not well-regulated.
The ikhtilaf is legitimate within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the mufta bihi position
The later scholars (e.g., in Radd al-Muhtar) recommend following the earlier scholars (mutaqaddimin) which corresponds to the view of the two. This is because their rules are well-ordered, more precautionary, and widely used in fatwas.
Thus, the operative ruling is that prayer is valid unless the meaning changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: وضع حرف موضع حرف آخر، فإن كانت الكلمة لا تخرج عن لفظ القرآن، ولم يتغير به المعنى المراد لا تفسد، كما لو قرأ: "إن الظالمون بواو الرفع أو قال: "والأرض وما دحها" مكان "طحها"، وإن خرجت به عن لفظ القرآن، ولم يتغير به المعنى لا تفسد عندهما خلافا لأبي يوسف، كما قرأ: "قيامين بالقسط" مكان قَوَّامِينَ، أو "دوارا" مكان وديارا، وإن لم تخرج به عن لفظ القرآن، وتغير به المعنى فالخلاف بالعكس، كما لو قرأ: "وأنتم خامدون" مكان سَامِدُونَ ، وللمتأخرين قواعد أخر غير ما ذكرنا، واقتصرنا على ما سبق، لاطرادها في كل الفروع، بخلاف قواعد المتأخرين وأعلم أنه لا يقيس مسائل زلة القارئ بعضها على بعض إلا من له دراية باللغة العربية والمعاني وغير ذلك مما يحتاج إليه التفسير، كما في "منية المصلي" وفي "النهر": وأحسن من لخص من كلامهم في زلة القارئ الكمال في زاد الفقير" فقال: إن كان الخطأ في الإعراب ولم يتغير به المعنى ككسر قواما مكان فتحها، وفتح باء ونعبد مكان ضمها لا تفسد، = المتأخرين فإن بعضهم يعتبر عسر الفصل بين الحرفين وعدمه، وبعضهم قرب المخرج وعدمه، ولكن الفروع غير منضبطة على شيء من ذلك، فالأولى الأخذ فيه بقول المتقدمين؛ لانضباط قواعدهم، وكون قولهم أحوط، وأكثر الفروع المذكورة في الفتاوى منزلة عليه. (رد المحتار)
Third issue: Putting one letter in place of another. If the word does not depart from the wording of the Quran and the intended meaning does not change, it does not invalidate, as if he read 'inna al-zalimun' with a waw of raf' or said 'wa al-ard wa ma dahaha' instead of 'tahaha'. If it departs from Quranic wording and the meaning does not change, it does not invalidate according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf (in one narration), as if he read 'qiyamin bi al-qist' instead of 'qawwamin' or 'dawaran' instead of 'diyaran'. If it does not depart from Quranic wording and the meaning changes, the difference is reversed, as if he read 'wa antum khamidun' instead of 'samidun'. The later scholars have other rules besides what we mentioned, but we limited to what is above because it applies consistently in all branches, unlike the rules of the later scholars. Know that one should not analogize among issues of 'zallat al-qari' except one who has knowledge of Arabic language, meanings, and other things that tafsir requires, as in 'Muniyat al-Musalli'. In 'al-Nahr': The best summary of their words on 'zallat al-qari' is by al-Kamal in 'Zad al-Faqir' who said: If the error is in i'rab and the meaning does not change, like kasr of 'qiwaman' instead of its fatha, and fatha of 'ba' of 'na'budu' instead of its damma, it does not invalidate. The later scholars: some consider difficulty of parting between two letters, others consider closeness of articulation point, but the branches are not regulated by any of that. So it is better to take the statement of the earlier scholars because their rules are well-ordered, their statement is more precautionary, and most of the branches mentioned in fatwas are built upon it. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the third mas'alah: substituting one letter for another, such as changing 'tahaha' to 'dahaha'. The author presents three scenarios: (1) The word remains Quranic and meaning unchanged: prayer is valid by agreement. Example: reading 'inna al-zalimun' with a waw instead of a damma (i'rab change but same word). (2) The word departs from Quranic wording but meaning does not change: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it does not invalidate, but Abu Yusuf has a differing view. Example: 'qiyamin' instead of 'qawwamin' (both mean 'upright'). (3) The word remains Quranic but meaning changes: then the difference is reversed, meaning Abu Yusuf and Muhammad might invalidate while Abu Hanifa? Actually the text says 'alkhilaf bil-'aks' which flips the positions. Example: 'khamidun' (extinguished) instead of 'samidun' (heedless). The author then states that later scholars have other rules, but the author focuses on the earlier ones because they are more universally applicable. The passage ends with a caution not to analogize among these cases without deep linguistic knowledge. The marginal note from Radd al-Muhtar discusses the later scholars' criteria (difficulty of parting, proximity of articulation) but ultimately recommends following the early scholars' well-defined rules as more precautionary and widely used in fatwas.

Key Terms
وضع حرف موضع حرف (waḍ' ḥarf mawḍi' ḥarf): Substituting one letter for another in a Quranic word
لفظ القرآن (lafẓ al-Qur'ān): The exact wording of the Quran as revealed

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person recites 'wa antum khamidun' instead of 'samidun', what is the ruling according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad)?
A) Prayer is invalid because meaning changed.
B) Prayer is valid because the word remains Quranic.
C) Prayer is invalid only if done intentionally.
D) Prayer is valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, substituting a letter in Quranic recitation invalidates the prayer only if the meaning changes, according to the relied-upon position.

Ruling on Incorrect Stopping and Starting in Recitation
Ruling on stopping and starting incorrectly during recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on incorrect waqf and ibtida' during prayer and apply the principle of hardship removal to justify non-invalidation.

Core Idea
Errors in stopping and starting do not invalidate prayer, even if meaning changes, due to removal of hardship.

Recitation in prayer requires proper stopping and starting, but mistakes are common, especially among laypeople. The Hanafi school, emphasizing ease, rules that such errors do not invalidate the prayer even when the meaning changes. This lesson examines the ruling, its basis in the principle of removing hardship (al-ḥaraj marfū‘), and the consensus of later Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: stopping (waqf) and starting (ibtidā') in incorrect places during Quran recitation in prayer. State the Hanafi ruling: the prayer remains valid (sahih) even if the meaning changes.
The ruling applies to both commoners and scholars, but the fatwa is general.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the legal principle that hardship is removed (al-ḥaraj marfū‘). The text references works like al-Dhakhira and al-Sirajiyya, and notes that strictness would cause hardship especially for commoners.
This is a qa'idah fiqhiyyah derived from Quran and Sunnah, used here as a rational proof (istihsan).
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Early Hanafi scholars differed: some held that if the meaning changes, the prayer becomes invalid. Later scholars (mutakhkhirin) overruled this, establishing the fatwa of non-invalidation in all cases.
The ikhtilaf is internal to the Hanafi school; the later view is mufta bihi.
4
Apply to sub-cases
Examine the text's examples: cutting a word (e.g., saying 'Al' instead of 'Alhamdu') and then continuing; stopping on condition before result; stopping on 'Wa qalati al-yahudu' and then starting the next verse. All are ruled non-invalidating.
Even egregious changes (like 'Shahida Allahu annahu la ilaha' then 'illa Huwa') do not invalidate.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that worshippers are not required to repeat prayers due to common recitation mistakes, reducing burden. This leniency aligns with the school's general tendency toward ease.
The ruling contrasts with stricter schools that may require repetition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب زلة القارئ
المسألة الثانية في الوقف والابتداء في غير موضعهما، فإن لم يتغير به المعنى لا تفسد بالإجماع من المتقدمين والمتأخرين، وإن تغير به المعنى ففيه اختلاف والفتوى على عدم الفساد بكل حال، وهو قول عامة علمائنا المتأخرين؛ لأن في مراعاة الوقف والوصل إيقاع الناس في الحرج لا سيما العوام، والحرج مرفوع، كما في "الذخيرة" و"السراجية" و"النصاب"، وفيه أيضًا لو ترك الوقف في جميع القرآن لا تفسد صلاته عندنا، وأما الحكم في قطع بعض الكلمة كما لو أراد أن يقول: ﴿الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ فقال: "ال"، فوقف على "اللام"، أو على "الحاء"، أو على "الميم"، أو أراد أن يقرأ وَالْعَادِيَاتِ فقال: "والعا" فوقف على "العين"، لانقطاع نفسه أو نسيان الباقي، ثم تمم، أو انتقل إلى آية أخرى، فالذي عليه عامة المشايخ عدم الفساد مطلقا وإن غير المعنى؛ للضرورة وعموم البلوى، كما في "الذخيرة"، وهو الأصح، كما ذكره أبو الليث.
عن
في غير موضعهما قال في "البزازية": الابتداء إن كان لا يغير المعنى تغيرا فاحشا لا يفسد، نحو: الوقف على الشرط قبل الجزاء، والابتداء بالجزاء، وكذا بين الصفة والموصوف، وإن غير المعنى نحو : وشَهِدَ اللَّهُ أَنَّهُ لا إِلَهَ (آل عمران: (۱۸) ثم ابتدأ إلَّا هُوَ لا يفسد عند عامة المشايخ؛ لأن العوام لا يميزون، ولو وقف على وَقَالَتِ الْيَهُودُ ثم ابتدأ بما بعده لا تفسد بالإجماع. (رد المحتار)
Section on the reciter's mistake. The second issue: stopping and starting in other than their proper places. If the meaning does not change, it does not invalidate by consensus of the early and later scholars. If the meaning changes, there is disagreement, and the fatwa is on non-invalidation in every case, which is the statement of the generality of our later scholars, because observing stopping and connecting places people in hardship especially the commoners, and hardship is removed, as in al-Dhakhira, al-Sirajiyya, and al-Nisab. Also in it: if he abandons stopping in all of the Quran, his prayer does not invalidate according to us. As for the ruling on cutting part of a word, such as if he wanted to say 'Alhamdu lillah' and said 'Al', and stopped on the lam, or on the ha, or on the mim, or he wanted to read 'Wal-'adiyat' and said 'Wal-'a' and stopped on the 'ayn, due to interruption of breath or forgetting the rest, then he completed or moved to another verse, that upon which the generality of the mashayikh is non-invalidation absolutely even if it changes the meaning, due to necessity and widespread occurrence, as in al-Dhakhira, and it is the most correct, as Abu al-Layth mentioned. About 'in other than their proper places', he said in al-Bazzaziyya: Starting, if it does not change the meaning egregiously, does not invalidate, such as stopping on the condition before the result, and starting with the result, and likewise between the adjective and the described. And if it changes the meaning, such as (wa shahida Allahu annahu la ilaha) then starting with (illa huwa), it does not invalidate according to the generality of the mashayikh because commoners do not distinguish. And if he stops on (wa qalati al-yahudu) then starts with what follows, it does not invalidate by consensus. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is a ruling from the section on the reciter's mistake (Zalla al-Qari). The second issue concerns stopping (waqf) and starting (ibtida') in places other than the appropriate ones. The ruling is that if the meaning does not change, the prayer is not invalidated by consensus. If the meaning changes, there is disagreement, but the relied-upon position (fatwa) is that it does not invalidate in any case, according to the majority of later Hanafi scholars. The reason given is that requiring correct stopping and starting would cause hardship (haraj) especially for common people, and hardship is removed in Islamic law. This principle is mentioned in works like al-Dhakhira and al-Sirajiyya. The text also rules on cutting a word (e.g., saying 'Al' instead of 'Alhamdu'): the majority of early mashayikh say it does not invalidate absolutely, even if meaning changes, due to necessity and widespread occurrence. A marginal note from al-Bazzaziyya further clarifies that even if meaning changes egregiously, it does not invalidate according to most mashayikh because commoners cannot distinguish. Stopping on 'وَقَالَتِ الْيَهُودُ' and then starting with what follows is valid by consensus. This shows the Hanafi school's leniency in matters of recitation errors during prayer.

Key Terms
زلة القارئ (zallat al-qāri’): Mistakes made by the reciter during prayer in recitation, including stopping, starting, or mispronunciation.
الوقف والابتداء (al-waqf wa al-ibtidā’): Stopping (waqf) at the end of a verse or phrase and starting (ibtidā’) a new recitation; rules about proper places.
الحرج مرفوع (al-ḥaraj marfū‘): The principle that hardship is removed in Islamic law, used here to justify leniency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a reciter stops on 'وَقَالَتِ الْيَهُودُ' and then starts with the next verse (changing the meaning), what is the ruling according to the text?
A) The prayer is invalid by consensus.
B) The prayer is not invalidated by consensus.
C) It depends on the reciter's knowledge.
D) It is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa holds that incorrect stopping and starting during prayer, even when changing the meaning, does not invalidate the prayer, based on the principle of removing hardship.

Ruling on Substituting One Letter for Another in Recitation
Detailed ruling on letter substitution in recitation with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on letter substitution in recitation and apply the three-scenario framework to determine the validity of prayer.

Core Idea
A letter substitution invalidates prayer only if it changes wording AND meaning; otherwise it's valid, with key intra-Hanafi differences.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses a common issue: when a reciter mistakenly substitutes one letter for another during prayer. The ruling is not uniform; it depends on two factors: whether the substituted word remains Quranic wording, and whether the intended meaning changes. The Hanafi school has nuanced positions, with disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side and Abu Yusuf on the other. Understanding this framework helps you evaluate similar cases and appreciate the precision of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key factors
The ruling depends on two conditions: (a) whether the word exits the Quranic wording, and (b) whether the meaning changes.
These are the independent variables that determine the outcome.
2
Classify the three scenarios
There are three combinations: (i) no exit, no meaning change; (ii) exit, no meaning change; (iii) no exit, meaning change.
The text provides examples for each.
3
Apply the ruling per scenario
For each scenario, state the ruling according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and note any disagreement with Abu Yusuf.
Scenario (i) is undisputed: valid. Scenario (ii): the two say valid, Abu Yusuf says invalid. Scenario (iii): the two say invalid, Abu Yusuf says valid.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf reversal
In scenario (ii) and (iii), the positions of the two and Abu Yusuf are reversed.
This shows the importance of which factor is prioritized.
5
Note the later scholars' approach
Later Hanafi scholars proposed additional rules based on difficulty of separation or proximity of makhraj, but the preferred view is to follow the early scholars (mutafaqqidim) for consistency and caution.
The text advises adopting the early scholars' view as mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة: وضع حرف موضع حرف آخر، فإن كانت الكلمة لا تخرج عن لفظ القرآن، ولم يتغير به المعنى المراد لا تفسد، كما لو قرأ: "إن الظالمون" بواو الرفع أو قال: "والأرض وما دحها" مكان "طحها"، وإن خرجت به عن لفظ القرآن، ولم يتغير به المعنى لا تفسد عندهما خلافا لأبي يوسف ، كما قرأ: "قيامين بالقسط" مكان قَوَّامِينَ، أو "دوارا" مكان ديارا، وإن لم تخرج به عن لفظ القرآن، وتغير به المعنى فالخلاف بالعكس، كما لو قرأ: "وأنتم خامدون" مكان سَامِدُونَ ، وللمتأخرين قواعد أخر غير ما ذكرنا، واقتصرنا على ما سبق، لاطرادها في كل الفروع، بخلاف قواعد المتأخرين وأعلم أنه لا يقيس مسائل زلة القارئ بعضها على بعض إلا من له دراية باللغة العربية والمعاني وغير ذلك مما يحتاج إليه التفسير، كما في "منية المصلي" وفي "النهر": وأحسن من لخص من كلامهم في زلة القارئ الكمال في زاد الفقير فقال: إن كان الخطأ في الإعراب ولم يتغير به المعنى ككسر قواما مكان فتحها ، وفتح باء ونعبد مكان ضمها لا تفسد. بے المتأخرين فإن بعضهم يعتبر عسر الفصل بين الحرفين وعدمه، وبعضهم قرب المخرج وعدمه، ولكن الفروع غير منضبطة على شيء من ذلك، فالأولى الأخذ فيه بقول المتقدمين؛ لانضباط قواعدهم، وكون قولهم أحوط، وأكثر الفروع المذكورة في الفتاوى منزلة عليه. (رد المحتار)
The third issue: placing a letter in the place of another letter. If the word does not exit the wording of the Quran, and the intended meaning does not change, it does not invalidate, such as if he reads 'Inna al-zalimun' with a waw of raf‘ or says 'Wa al-ard wa ma dahaha' instead of 'tahaha'. And if it exits the wording of the Quran, and the meaning does not change, it does not invalidate according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf, such as if he reads 'Qayyamin bil-qist' instead of 'Qawwamin', or 'Dawaran' instead of 'Diyaran'. And if it does not exit the wording of the Quran, and the meaning changes, the disagreement is opposite, such as if he reads 'Wa antum khamidun' instead of 'Samidun'. For the later scholars there are other rules besides what we mentioned, and we confined ourselves to what preceded, because of their universal applicability to all branches, unlike the rules of the later scholars. Know that no one should draw analogies between the issues of the reciter's mistake except one who has expertise in the Arabic language and meanings and other things needed for tafsir, as in Munyat al-Musalli and in al-Nahr: The best summary of their words on zallat al-qari is al-Kamal in Zad al-Faqir, who said: If the error is in i‘rab and the meaning does not change, such as breaking the qaf of 'qiwaman' instead of opening it, or opening the ba of 'wa na‘budu' instead of damma, it does not invalidate. For the later scholars, some consider the difficulty of separating between the two letters and its absence, and some consider the closeness of the makhraj and its absence, but the branches are not regulated on any of that. So it is better to adopt the statement of the early scholars due to the regulation of their rules and their statement being more precautionary, and most of the branches mentioned in the fatawa are based on it. (Radd al-Muhtar)
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is the third issue in the section on the reciter's mistake: substituting one letter for another. The ruling depends on whether the word remains Quranic wording and whether the meaning changes. The text presents three scenarios: (1) If the word does not exit Quranic wording and meaning does not change, prayer is valid (e.g., using raf‘ instead of nasb on 'al-zalimun' or mispronouncing 'dahaha' for 'tahaha'). (2) If the word exits Quranic wording but meaning does not change, there is an intra-Hanafi disagreement: according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), it does not invalidate; Abu Yusuf says it does. Examples include 'qayyamin' for 'qawwamin' or 'dawaran' for 'diyaran'. (3) If the word does not exit Quranic wording but meaning changes, the disagreement is reversed: now Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it does invalidate, while Abu Yusuf says it does not. Example: 'khamidun' instead of 'samidun'. Later Hanafi scholars formulated additional rules, but the text advises adopting the early scholars' views because they are consistent and more precautionary. The text also warns against qiyas (analogy) in these matters except for those well-versed in Arabic and tafsir.

Key Terms
زلة القارئ (zallat al-qāri’): Mistakes of the reciter, here specifically substitution of letters.
لفظ القرآن (lafẓ al-Qur’ān): The exact wording of the Quran; exiting it means the recited word is no longer Quranic text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling if a reciter substitutes a letter causing the word to exit Quranic wording but meaning remains unchanged?
A) The prayer is invalid.
B) The prayer is valid.
C) The reciter must perform sajdah al-sahw.
D) The recitation must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
The validity of prayer after a letter substitution depends on whether the word remains Quranic and the meaning changes; the preferred Hanafi view adopts the early scholars' position for consistency.

Actions That Do Not Invalidate the Prayer
Rulings on actions that do not invalidate prayer with intra-Hanafi dispute on recitation errors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on actions that do not invalidate the prayer according to Hanafi fiqh and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on recitation errors.

Core Idea
Prayer remains valid despite looking at text, eating less than a chickpea, and minor recitation errors.

This passage from Nur al-Idah, supplemented by Hashiyat al-Tahtawi, addresses common concerns about prayer invalidation. It clarifies that certain actions—like looking at written text, swallowing small food residue, and even making recitation mistakes—generally do not break the prayer. However, there is a notable difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars regarding the severity of recitation errors, with later scholars adopting a more lenient stance. Understanding these rulings helps avoid unnecessary repetition of prayers and accommodates the realities of non-Arab reciters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions and their base rulings
Examine the passage to list the specific actions that are said not to invalidate prayer: looking at text and understanding it, eating food between teeth if less than a chickpea, and recitation changes that do not alter meaning (or even if they do, according to later scholars). Note the general ruling: the prayer remains valid (sahih).
The chickpea (حِمَّصَة) is the threshold used in Hanafi fiqh for food consumption during prayer and fast.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on recitation errors
Identify the point of contention: earlier Hanafi scholars (مُتَقَدِّمُون) held that changing case endings (e.g., in آیه فاطر ۲۸) invalidates the prayer, while later scholars (مُتَأَخِّرُون) like Ibn al-Fadl and Ibn Muqatil ruled it does not. Notice that the text prioritizes the more lenient view (قول هؤلاء أوسع).
This dispute is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; both positions are based on Hanafi methodology.
3
Analyze the later scholars' rationale
Understand that the later scholars considered that common errors by non-Arabs (e.g., saying 'wa yaka na'budu' instead of 'iyyaka na'budu') do not fundamentally change the essential meaning and would cause undue hardship if invalidated. They apply istihsan (juristic preference) to alleviate difficulty.
This flexibility reflects the Hanafi principle of accommodating prevalent custom (urf) and necessity.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference is that according to the later (mufta bihi) view, prayers behind an imam who makes such slips remain valid. This is the operative ruling for fatwa, making worship easier for the community.
The earlier stricter view is still historically recognized but not applied in practice.
5
Apply the threshold and conditions for food consumption
The passage and commentary specify that only food already between the teeth (a residue) is exempt if less than a chickpea. Swallowing external food (even a sesame seed) or a raindrop knowingly invalidates both prayer and fast. Confirm that the chickpea threshold for nullification applies to both worship acts.
This shows the interconnectedness of fiqh rulings across different acts of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما لا يفسد الصلاة] ما لا يفسد الصلاة لو نظر المصلي إلى مكتوب وفهمه أو أكل ما بين أسنانه وكان دون الحمصة قرآنا كان أو غيره وإن غير كنصب همزة العُلَمَاءُ وضم هاء الجلالة من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّمَا يَخْشَى اللَّهَ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ الْعُلَمَاءُ (فاطر: (۲۸ تفسد على قول المتقدمين واختلف المتأخرون فقال ابن الفضل وابن مقاتل وأبو جعفر والحلواني وابن سلام وإسماعيل الزاهدي لا تفسد وقول هؤلاء أوسع. وإن كان بوضع حرف مكان حرف، ولم يتغير المعنى، نحو: "أياب" مكان أوَّابٌ لا تفسد، وعن أبي سعيد تفسد وكثيرا ما يقع في قراءة بعض القرويين والأتراك والسودان "وياك نعبد" بواو مكان الهمزة، و"الصراط الذين" بزيادة الألف واللام، وصرحوا في الصورتين بعدم الفساد وإن غير المعنى، وتمامه فيه فليراجع، والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم، وأستغفر الله العظيم. ما بين أسنانه: قيده به؛ لأنه لو تناول شيئاً من خارج ولو سمسمة، أو قطرة مطر فوصلت إلى حلقه، فسدت وصومه إذا كان ذاكراً. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٤١] وكان دون الحمصة : أما إذا كان قدر الحمصة فأكثر أفسدها، كما يفسد الصوم، فما يفسدها يفسده، وما لا فلا. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٤١]
Section on what does not invalidate prayer: The prayer is not invalidated if the praying person looks at a written text and understands it, or eats what is between his teeth and it is less than a chickpea, whether it is Quran or other. And if he changes [the recitation] like by putting a fathah on the hamzah of al-‘ulama’ and a dammah on the ha’ of jalalah in the verse ‘Indeed, only those fear Allah among His servants who are knowledgeable’ (Fatir 28): it invalidates according to the earlier scholars, but the later scholars differed; Ibn al-Fadl, Ibn Muqatil, Abu Ja‘far, al-Halwani, Ibn Salam, and Isma‘il al-Zahidi said it does not invalidate, and their view is more lenient. And if he puts a letter in place of another letter and the meaning does not change, like ‘ayab’ instead of awwab, it does not invalidate, and from Abu Sa‘id it invalidates. And it often occurs in the recitation of some villagers, Turks, and Sudanese: ‘wa yaka na‘budu’ with a waw instead of the hamzah, and ‘al-sirata alladhina’ with extra alif lam, and they explicitly stated in both cases that there is no invalidation even if the meaning changes, and its completion is therein so refer to it. And Allah knows best, and I seek forgiveness from Allah the Mighty. ‘What is between his teeth’: he restricted it with this; because if he takes something from outside, even a sesame seed, or a raindrop that reaches his throat, his prayer and his fast are invalidated if he remembers [that he is praying/fasting]. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi: 341] ‘And it is less than a chickpea’: as for when it is the size of a chickpea or more, it invalidates it, just as it invalidates the fast; so whatever invalidates the fast invalidates it, and whatever does not does not. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi: 341]
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah, with commentary from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi, addresses actions that do not invalidate the prayer. The primary ruling: looking at a written text and understanding it, or eating food residue from between the teeth if less than a chickpea (about the size of a dried chickpea), does not invalidate the prayer, whether the text is Quranic or not. The passage then discusses the issue of changing letters during Quran recitation in prayer. The earlier scholars held that if a recitation error alters important grammatical features like case endings (e.g., changing the nominative 'al-'ulama' to an accusative by mistake), the prayer is invalidated. Later scholars, including several authoritative Hanafi jurists like Ibn al-Fadl and Ibn Muqatil, ruled that such mistakes do not invalidate, and this lenient view is preferred because it avoids hardship. The commentary specifies that if the meaning does not change (like replacing 'awwab' with 'ayab', both meaning 'oft-returning'), the prayer remains valid according to most, though Abu Sa'id reported invalidation. Common errors like saying 'wa yaka na'budu' (with waw instead of hamza) or 'al-sirata alladhina' (with extra definite article) are explicitly excused even if the meaning changes, as per the later view. The final part clarifies the 'between the teeth' condition: only residue already present is covered; swallowing external substances like a sesame seed or raindrop knowingly invalidates both prayer and fast. The chickpea threshold aligns with similar rules in fasting: if the eaten item equals or exceeds a chickpea, the act of worship is invalidated.

Key Terms
الحمصة (al-ḥimmaṣah): A chickpea; used as a measure for the amount of food that invalidates prayer or fast if swallowed.
المتقدمون والمتأخرون (al-mutaqaddimūn wal-muta'akhkhirūn): Earlier and later Hanafi scholars; here, the earlier scholars held stricter views on recitation errors, while later scholars adopted a more lenient position.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling on a praying person who mistakenly changes a grammatical case (like the final vowel) in a Quran verse?
A) The prayer is invalidated because the meaning changed.
B) The prayer remains valid according to the later scholars, and this is the relied-upon view.
C) It is disliked but valid only if the meaning did not change.
D) It is only invalidated if done intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Prayer is not invalidated by looking at text, eating less than a chickpea, or minor recitation errors; the later Hanafi view on recitation errors is the operative position for fatwa.

Disliked Actions in Prayer (Part 2): Garment, Recitation, and Movements
Continuation of makruhat list with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disliked actions (makruhat) related to garment, recitation, and minor movements in Hanafi prayer, and explain which conditions remove the dislike or may lead to invalidation.

Core Idea
A prayer's perfection is reduced by disliked acts, but only excessive unrelated actions break its validity.

This lesson continues from the earlier list of makruhat in prayer, focusing on how one wears their garment, recites, and performs small movements. Understanding these rulings helps the worshipper avoid actions that diminish the reward of prayer while recognizing that most do not invalidate the prayer itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the action category
Classify each action as garment-related (e.g., sadl, kaf al-thawb), recitation-related (e.g., repeating surah, mixing order), or movement-related (e.g., fanning, stretching).
2
Determine the default ruling
For most listed items, the default ruling is makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful or invalidating).
Some items like excessive fanning may have a stronger ruling; see al-Dhakhira.
3
Check for exceptions
Many disliked actions become permissible or not disliked when there is a valid reason, such as closing eyes to focus or using a garment for warmth in extreme cold.
The text explicitly mentions exceptions for sadl (with excuse) and closing eyes (to aid concentration).
4
Assess severity and consequences
Distinguish between actions that are merely disliked and those that may invalidate prayer (e.g., fanning excessively or performing multiple unrelated little actions). The concept of 'amal qalil vs. 'amal kathir is key.
According to al-Dhakhira, fanning with a fan invalidates even if not repeated, unlike fanning with a sleeve.

ما يكره للمصلي وكف ثوبه وسدله والاندراج فيه بحيث لا يخرج يديه، وجعل الثوب تحت إبطه الأيمن، وطرح جانبيه على عاتقه الأيسر، والقراءة في غير حالة القيام، وإطالة الركعة أو عكسه كإتمام القراءة حالة الركوع الأولى في التطوع، وتطويل الثانية على الأولى في جميع الصلوات، وتكرار السورة في ركعة واحدة من الفرض وقراءة سورة فوق التي قرأها وفصله بسورة بين سورتين قرأهما في ركعتين وشم طيب وترويحه بثوبه، أو مروحة مرة أو مرتين، وتحويل السورتين قصداً أصابع يديه أو رجليه عن القبلة في السجود وغيره، وترك وضع اليدين على الركبتين في الركوع، والتثاؤب، وتغميض عينيه، ورفعهما للسماء والتمطي، والعمل القليل،

وكف ثوبه أي رفعه بين يديه أو من خلفه إذا أراد السجود وقيل أن يجمع ثوبه ويشده في وسطه. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٠] وسدله هو في الشرع الإرسال بدون لبس معتاد، مثلا يجعل الثوب على رأسه وكتفيه، أو كتفيه فقط، ويُرسل جوانبه من غير أن يضمها . [ مراقي الفلاح: ۳۵۰] وهذا إذا كان بغير عذر، أما بالعذر كبرد وحر شديد فلا يكره . [ حاشية الطحطاوي: ۳۵۰] في التطوع أما في الفرض؛ فإنه مسنون إجماعاً في صلاة الفجر، وكذا في غير الفجر عند محمد، وعليه الفتوى [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥١] وقراءة سورة إلخ كمن قرأ في الأولى سورة الإخلاص، وفي الثانية سورة لهب قال ابن مسعود ه: من قرأ القرءآن منكوساً، فهو منكوس وما شرع لتعليم الأطفال إلا لتيسير الحفظ بقصر السور. [مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٢] وفصله بسورة وقال بعضهم لا يكره إذا كانت السورة طويلة كما لو كان بينهما سورتان قصيرتان. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٢] وترويحه: هو جلب الروح بفتح الراء نسيم الريح مروحة إلخ: بكسر الميم وفتح الواو: آلة يحرك بها الريح؛ ليتبرد به عند اشتداد الحر، يقال لها في الهندية: پنها، والجمع: مراوح. [مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٣] مرة أو مرتين هذا بناء منه على أن العمل الكثير ثلاث حركات والقليل دون ذلك، والذي في "الذخيرة": أنها تفسد بالمروحة وإن لم يتكرر بخلاف الكم. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٣] وتغميض عينيه إلخ: أطلقه بغير مصلحة كما إذا غمضهما لرؤية ما يمنع خشوعه فلا كراهة. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٤] والتمطي: أي التمدد وهو مد يديه وإبداء ،صدره والعامة يخطؤون بإبدال يائه عيناً. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٤] والعمل القليل أطلقه وهو مقيد بالمنافي للصلاة، وأفراده كثيرة: كنتف شعرة، ومنه الرمية عن القوس مرةً في صلاة الخوف، كالمشي في صلاته. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٥] أما المطلوب في الصلاة فهو منها كتحريك الأصابع لعد التسبيح في صلاته. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٥]
What is disliked for the person praying: folding his garment, letting it hang loosely, wrapping himself in it without taking out his hands, placing the garment under his right armpit, throwing its ends over his left shoulder, reciting in other than standing position, lengthening the rak'ah or its opposite like completing recitation in ruku' in the first rak'ah of nafl, lengthening the second over the first in all prayers, repeating a surah in one rak'ah of fard, reciting a surah higher than the one he read, separating with a surah between two surahs read in two rak'ahs, smelling perfume, fanning himself with his garment or a fan once or twice, intentionally changing surahs, pointing his fingers or feet away from the qibla in prostration and otherwise, not placing his hands on his knees in ruku', yawning, closing his eyes, raising them to the sky, stretching, and little action. Commentary: ... (explanations of each).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This lesson continues the list of makruhat in prayer. Key points include: 
- Garment issues: Folding (kaf) and hanging (sadl) are disliked unless for necessity; proper dress is recommended. 
- Recitation: Reciting while not standing, lengthening the first rak'ah of nafl excessively, lengthening the second over the first in all prayers, repeating a surah in fard, or reciting in reverse order (e.g., reading a later surah before an earlier one) are disliked. The commentary notes that reversing surahs is makruh, except for teaching children. 
- Actions: Fanning oneself with a garment or fan once or twice is makruh; if done excessively it may invalidate (according to al-Dhakhira). Closing eyes without benefit is makruh; if it helps concentration, it is not. Stretching and little actions are disliked if they contradict the spirit of prayer. 
- The concept of 'amal qalil (little action) is defined: it is makruh if it contradicts prayer, but actions that are part of prayer (like moving fingers for counting tasbih) are permissible.

Key Terms
السدل (al-sadl): Letting the garment hang loosely without proper wearing, e.g., placing it on the head or shoulders without wrapping.
العمل القليل (al-‘amal al-qalīl): Little action: a small movement that is not part of the prayer, such as plucking a hair or shooting an arrow once in fear prayer.
التمطي (al-tamaṭṭī): Stretching: extending the arms and exposing the chest.
كف الثوب (kaf al-thawb): Folding or gathering the garment to raise it when prostrating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible and not disliked
B) Makruh tanzihi
C) Invalidates the prayer
D) Sunnah

Key Takeaway
Most disliked actions in prayer are makruh tanzihi; they reduce reward but do not invalidate, except excessive unrelated actions which may break the prayer.

Makruhat of Dress in Prayer
Listing disliked dress actions with definitions and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disliked actions related to dress in prayer according to the Hanafi school and state the exceptions for excuse.

Core Idea
Letting a garment hang loosely (sadl) is makruh without excuse, but permissible in extreme heat or cold.

This lesson focuses on the specific ways of wearing garments during prayer that the Hanafi school considers disliked. The rulings aim to preserve humility and focus. The commentary clarifies the definitions of key terms like 'sadl' and 'indiraj' and notes when necessity overrides the dislike.

Guided Path
1
Identify each disliked dress action
From the passage, list the actions: kaff (folding away), sadl (letting hang), indiraj (wrapping without hand access), placing garment under right armpit, throwing sides over left shoulder.
These are specific modes of wearing the garment that depart from customary modesty.
2
Define each action using commentary
Use the commentary from Maraqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi to understand the precise meaning of each term, e.g., sadl is letting the garment hang without gathering.
The commentary provides the legal definitions.
3
Apply the exception for excuse
Note that the dislike is conditional on the absence of a legitimate excuse, such as extreme cold or heat. With an excuse, the action is not disliked.
This is from the commentary: 'with an excuse... it is not disliked.'
4
Distinguish between disliked and invalidating
These actions are makruh tanzihi (disliked but not sinful enough to invalidate prayer). They are not mubtilat unless they involve excessive movement that breaks the prayer's continuity.
None of these dress actions are said to invalidate prayer per se.

[حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٥] وهو مقيد
Book of Prayer. 119. Disliked for the worshiper: folding his garment, and letting it hang (sadl), and wrapping himself in it such that his hands do not come out, and placing the garment under his right armpit, and throwing its two sides over his left shoulder, and reciting in other than the standing position, and lengthening the first rak'ah in supererogatory prayer, and making the second longer than the first in all prayers, and repeating a surah in one rak'ah of obligatory prayer, and reciting a surah higher than the one he recited, and separating between two surahs he recited in two rak'ahs by [another] surah, and smelling perfume and fanning oneself with his garment or a fan once or twice, and turning the fingers of his hands or feet away from the qibla in prostration and otherwise, and leaving placing the hands on the knees in bowing, and yawning, and closing his eyes, and raising them to the sky, and stretching, and slight action. [Commentary:] Kaff (folding) of his garment: lifting it in front of him or behind when he wants to prostrate; it is said to gather his garment and tie it around his waist. [Maraqi al-Falah: 350] Sadl: in Sacred Law, letting it hang without customary wearing; for example, he places the garment on his head and shoulders, or just his shoulders, and lets its sides hang without gathering them. [Maraqi al-Falah: 350] And this is if without excuse; but with an excuse like severe cold or heat, it is not disliked. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 350] In supererogatory prayer; regarding obligatory prayer, it is sunnah by consensus in Fajr prayer, and likewise in other than Fajr according to Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 351] And reciting a surah etc.: like one who recites in the first [rak'ah] Surat al-Ikhlas and in the second Surat Lahab. Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: Whoever recites the Quran inverted, he is inverted. It was only prescribed for teaching children to facilitate memorization by shortening surahs. [Maraqi al-Falah: 352] And separating by a surah: some said it is not disliked if the surah is long, like if there are two short surahs between them. [Maraqi al-Falah: 352] And fanning (tarwih): bringing air (ar-rih) with the garment, or a fan (mirwaha): an instrument moved to produce wind to cool oneself in intense heat; in Hindi it is called 'pankha', plural 'marawih'. [Maraqi al-Falah: 353] Once or twice: this is based on the position that much action is three movements and little is less than that. In al-Dhakhirah: it invalidates [the prayer] with a fan even if not repeated, unlike [with] the sleeve. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 353] And closing his eyes etc.: He [the author] made it unrestricted [in dislike] without benefit; if he closes them to avoid seeing something that would break his humility, then there is no dislike. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 354] And stretching (tamatti): extending, i.e., stretching his hands and expanding his chest; the common people err by substituting 'ayn for its ya'. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 354] And slight action: He made it unrestricted, but it is restricted to what contravenes the prayer; its instances are many: like plucking a hair, and shooting an arrow once in the prayer of fear, like walking in his prayer. [Maraqi al-Falah: 355] As for what is required in prayer, it is part of it, like moving fingers to count tasbih in his prayer. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 355] And it is restricted.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating various actions that are disliked (makruh) during prayer according to the Hanafi school. The text lists actions related to dress, posture, recitation, and minor movements. The accompanying commentary from Maraqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies the definitions and conditions of each ruling. For example, 'sadl' (letting a garment hang loosely) is defined and exemptions are given for extreme weather. Lengthening the first rak'ah in supererogatory prayer is disliked, whereas in obligatory prayer (especially Fajr) it is sunnah. Reciting the Quran in reverse order is disliked but allowed for teaching. Fanning oneself is considered little action but may invalidate the prayer if with a fan. The commentary restricts the dislike of closing eyes to cases without benefit. Slight action that contravenes the prayer invalidates it, but actions that are part of the prayer itself, like moving fingers for counting tasbih, are excepted.

Key Terms
السدل (as-sadl): Letting a garment hang loosely without wearing it in the customary manner, e.g., placing it on the head and shoulders and letting the sides hang without gathering.
الاندراج (al-indiraj): Wrapping oneself in the garment such that the hands cannot come out, resembling being enclosed.
الكف (al-kaff): Folding or gathering the garment, e.g., lifting it when prostrating or tying it around the waist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on 'sadl' during prayer?
A) It is permissible (mubah) in all cases.
B) It is disliked (makruh) only if there is no valid excuse.
C) It is forbidden (haram).
D) It is obligatory to avoid it.

Key Takeaway
Dress actions in prayer are makruh only when done without a legitimate excuse like extreme weather.

Makruhat of Recitation and Posture in Prayer
Listing disliked recitation and posture actions with commentary on exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the disliked actions related to recitation and posture in prayer according to the Hanafi school and explain the rationale for each.

Core Idea
Reciting the Quran in reverse order is disliked but permitted for teaching; lengthening the first rak'ah in supererogatory prayer is makruh.

The Hanafi school specifies certain recitation and posture actions that are disliked during prayer to maintain focus and reverence. These include reciting while not standing, making the second rak'ah longer than the first, repeating a surah in obligatory prayer, and actions like yawning, closing eyes, and stretching. The commentary provides definitions and exceptions.

Guided Path
1
List disliked recitation actions
From the passage, enumerate: reciting in other than standing, lengthening first rak'ah in supererogatory, making second longer than first in all prayers, repeating a surah in obligatory prayer, reciting a higher surah after a lower one, separating two surahs with another surah.
These are all detailed in the passage.
2
List disliked posture actions
Include: leaving hands off knees in bowing, yawning, closing eyes without benefit, raising eyes to sky, stretching.
These are also enumerated.
3
Apply the exceptions and commentary
Note: reciting in reverse order is disliked but allowed for teaching children. Lengthening first rak'ah is for supererogatory; for obligatory (Fajr) it's sunnah by consensus. Closing eyes without benefit is disliked, but if to avoid distraction, it's not disliked.
The commentary provides these nuances.
4
Explain the wisdom behind the rulings
These rulings aim to preserve the proper order and focus of prayer. For example, lengthening the first rak'ah may lead to impatience; repeating surahs may cause monotony; yawning and stretching indicate laziness.
Not explicitly in the text but derived from fiqh principles.

Source Text

كتاب الصلاة ۱۱۹ ما يكره للمصلي وكف ثوبه وسدله والاندراج فيه بحيث لا يخرج يديه، وجعل الثوب تحت إبطه الأيمن، وطرح جانبيه على عاتقه الأيسر، والقراءة في غير حالة القيام، وإطالة الركعة الأولى في التطوع، وتطويل الثانية على الأولى في جميع الصلوات، وتكرار السورة في ركعة واحدة من الفرض وقراءة سورة فوق التي قرأها وفصله بسورة بين سورتين قرأهما في ركعتين وشم طيب وترويحه بثوبه، أو مروحة مرة أو مرتين، وتحويل أصابع يديه أو رجليه عن القبلة في السجود وغيره، وترك وضع اليدين على الركبتين في الركوع، والتثاؤب، وتغميض عينيه، ورفعهما للسماء والتمطي، والعمل القليل، وكف ثوبه أي رفعه بين يديه أو من خلفه إذا أراد السجود وقيل أن يجمع ثوبه ويشده في وسطه. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٠] وسدله هو في الشرع الإرسال بدون لبس معتاد، مثلا يجعل الثوب على رأسه وكتفيه، أو كتفيه فقط، ويُرسل جوانبه من غير أن يضمها . [ مراقي الفلاح: ۳۵۰] وهذا إذا كان بغير عذر، أما بالعذر كبرد وحر شديد فلا يكره . [ حاشية الطحطاوي: ۳۵۰] في التطوع أما في الفرض؛ فإنه مسنون إجماعاً في صلاة الفجر، وكذا في غير الفجر عند محمد، وعليه الفتوى [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥١] وقراءة سورة إلخ كمن قرأ في الأولى سورة الإخلاص، وفي الثانية سورة لهب قال ابن مسعود ه: من قرأ القرءآن منكوساً، فهو منكوس وما شرع لتعليم الأطفال إلا لتيسير الحفظ بقصر السور. [مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٢] وفصله بسورة وقال بعضهم لا يكره إذا كانت السورة طويلة كما لو كان بينهما سورتان قصيرتان. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٢] وترويحه: هو جلب الروح بفتح الراء نسيم الريح مروحة إلخ: بكسر الميم وفتح الواو: آلة يحرك بها الريح؛ ليتبرد به عند اشتداد الحر، يقال لها في الهندية: پنها، والجمع: مراوح. [مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٣] مرة أو مرتين هذا بناء منه على أن العمل الكثير ثلاث حركات والقليل دون ذلك، والذي في "الذخيرة": أنها تفسد بالمروحة وإن لم يتكرر بخلاف الكم. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٣] وتغميض عينيه إلخ: أطلقه بغير مصلحة كما إذا غمضهما لرؤية ما يمنع خشوعه فلا كراهة. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٤] والتمطي: أي التمدد وهو مد يديه وإبداء صدره والعامة يخطؤون بإبدال يائه عيناً. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٤] والعمل القليل أطلقه وهو مقيد بالمنافي للصلاة، وأفراده كثيرة: كنتف شعرة، ومنه الرمية عن القوس مرةً في صلاة الخوف، كالمشي في صلاته. مراقي الفلاح: ٣٥٥] أما المطلوب في الصلاة فهو منها كتحريك الأصابع لعد التسبيح في صلاته. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٣٥٥] وهو مقيد
Book of Prayer. 119. Disliked for the worshiper: folding his garment, and letting it hang (sadl), and wrapping himself in it such that his hands do not come out, and placing the garment under his right armpit, and throwing its two sides over his left shoulder, and reciting in other than the standing position, and lengthening the first rak'ah in supererogatory prayer, and making the second longer than the first in all prayers, and repeating a surah in one rak'ah of obligatory prayer, and reciting a surah higher than the one he recited, and separating between two surahs he recited in two rak'ahs by [another] surah, and smelling perfume and fanning oneself with his garment or a fan once or twice, and turning the fingers of his hands or feet away from the qibla in prostration and otherwise, and leaving placing the hands on the knees in bowing, and yawning, and closing his eyes, and raising them to the sky, and stretching, and slight action. [Commentary:] Kaff (folding) of his garment: lifting it in front of him or behind when he wants to prostrate; it is said to gather his garment and tie it around his waist. [Maraqi al-Falah: 350] Sadl: in Sacred Law, letting it hang without customary wearing; for example, he places the garment on his head and shoulders, or just his shoulders, and lets its sides hang without gathering them. [Maraqi al-Falah: 350] And this is if without excuse; but with an excuse like severe cold or heat, it is not disliked. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 350] In supererogatory prayer; regarding obligatory prayer, it is sunnah by consensus in Fajr prayer, and likewise in other than Fajr according to Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 351] And reciting a surah etc.: like one who recites in the first [rak'ah] Surat al-Ikhlas and in the second Surat Lahab. Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) said: Whoever recites the Quran inverted, he is inverted. It was only prescribed for teaching children to facilitate memorization by shortening surahs. [Maraqi al-Falah: 352] And separating by a surah: some said it is not disliked if the surah is long, like if there are two short surahs between them. [Maraqi al-Falah: 352] And fanning (tarwih): bringing air (ar-rih) with the garment, or a fan (mirwaha): an instrument moved to produce wind to cool oneself in intense heat; in Hindi it is called 'pankha', plural 'marawih'. [Maraqi al-Falah: 353] Once or twice: this is based on the position that much action is three movements and little is less than that. In al-Dhakhirah: it invalidates [the prayer] with a fan even if not repeated, unlike [with] the sleeve. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 353] And closing his eyes etc.: He [the author] made it unrestricted [in dislike] without benefit; if he closes them to avoid seeing something that would break his humility, then there is no dislike. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 354] And stretching (tamatti): extending, i.e., stretching his hands and expanding his chest; the common people err by substituting 'ayn for its ya'. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 354] And slight action: He made it unrestricted, but it is restricted to what contravenes the prayer; its instances are many: like plucking a hair, and shooting an arrow once in the prayer of fear, like walking in his prayer. [Maraqi al-Falah: 355] As for what is required in prayer, it is part of it, like moving fingers to count tasbih in his prayer. [Hashiyat al-Tahtawi: 355] And it is restricted.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement enumerating various actions that are disliked (makruh) during prayer according to the Hanafi school. The text lists actions related to dress, posture, recitation, and minor movements. The accompanying commentary from Maraqi al-Falah and Hashiyat al-Tahtawi clarifies the definitions and conditions of each ruling. For example, 'sadl' (letting a garment hang loosely) is defined and exemptions are given for extreme weather. Lengthening the first rak'ah in supererogatory prayer is disliked, whereas in obligatory prayer (especially Fajr) it is sunnah. Reciting the Quran in reverse order is disliked but allowed for teaching. Fanning oneself is considered little action but may invalidate the prayer if with a fan. The commentary restricts the dislike of closing eyes to cases without benefit. Slight action that contravenes the prayer invalidates it, but actions that are part of the prayer itself, like moving fingers for counting tasbih, are excepted.

Key Terms
التثاؤب (at-tatha'ub): Yawning, which is disliked during prayer as it indicates slackness.
التمطي (at-tamatti): Stretching the body, especially extending hands and expanding chest, showing laziness.

Recitation and posture actions are makruh with specific exceptions for necessity and benefit.

Salah — Prayer
